We need N such that if d1>d2>d3 are the three largest proper divisors, then d1+d2+d3 = N. Also, note that d1 is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor (since the largest proper divisor is N/p). This is reminiscent of "semiperfect numbers" or "pseudoperfect numbers"? Actually, a number N is called "semiperfect" if it is equal to the sum of some of its proper divisors. Here we require the sum of the three largest proper divisors to equal N. This is a stronger condition. We need to find all N such that N = sum of three largest proper divisors. Then these N are fixed points of f, so they are valid starting values (since then the sequence becomes constant). Also, maybe there are cycles of length >1 that are valid. For instance, if a_1 leads to a_2, and a_2 leads back to a_1, and both have at least three proper divisors, then that would be a valid infinite sequence. But the problem asks: Determine all possible values of a_1. So a_1 could be any fixed point, i.e., any number of the form 2 * 3^t * m where t ≥ 1 and m is a product of primes ≥7 (including 1). However, we also need to consider whether there are other possibilities where the sequence does not become constant but still infinite, i.e., cycles. For example, could a_1 lead to a_2, and a_2 leads back to a_1, and both have at least three proper divisors? Then the sequence would be periodic. So we need to check if there exist cycles of length >1 that stay within the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors.

Given the recursion f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. We need to find all N such that the orbit under f never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Possibly some numbers may lead to a fixed point eventually, and then the sequence becomes constant. So a_1 could be any number that eventually reaches a fixed point (or perhaps a cycle) without ever hitting a number with fewer than three proper divisors. But we need to determine all possible a_1 for which an infinite sequence exists. Since the recursion is deterministic, the condition is that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. So a_1 must be such that its orbit under f stays in the set S = {positive integers with at least three proper divisors}. So we need to characterize the set of numbers that are "good" (i.e., their orbits never leave S). This is a kind of "attractor" set. So we need to find all numbers N such that the orbit under f never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since f is deterministic, we can try to characterize the "good" numbers.

Observation: If N is not divisible by 3, then f(N) < N. If N is divisible by 3, then f(N) ≥ N, with equality only for numbers of the form 2*3^a * m where m is product of primes ≥7 and no factor 5. Also, note that if N is divisible by 3 and not of that special form, then f(N) > N. So the sequence increases when it is divisible by 3 and not fixed. Since it increases, eventually it will become larger than some bound. Could it increase without ever hitting a number with fewer than three proper divisors? Possibly, if it always remains composite and not a prime square. But we need to examine the behavior.

Let's try to understand f(N) for numbers divisible by 3. Write N = 6k? Actually, if N is divisible by 3 and 2, then N divisible by 6. If N is divisible by 3 but not by 2 (i.e., odd), then N is odd and divisible by 3. But then the largest proper divisor is N/3 (since smallest prime factor is 3). For odd numbers divisible by 3, we need to have at least three proper divisors. Now, for even numbers, we need to consider the exponent of 2. If N is divisible by 4 (i.e., exponent of 2 at least 2), then N/2 is a proper divisor, and N/4 is also a proper divisor if 4 divides N. So sum depends on relative sizes.

Goal: Determine all a_1 for which the sequence is infinite and all terms have at least three proper divisors. Possibly only numbers that are "good" in the sense that the recursion leads to a fixed point or cycles that stay within the allowed set. We need to characterize all possible a_1.

We need to see if S(N) itself has at least three proper divisors, and if we can continue.

We need to systematically determine all numbers N such that the orbit under f never leaves S. This is a problem about the dynamics of f. Since f(N) is the sum of the three largest proper divisors, we can think about the "stable" numbers. Observations: If N is not divisible by 3, then f(N) < N (as we saw from many examples, but need to prove). Since f is deterministic, we can try to characterize the "good" numbers.

Observation: If N is not divisible by 3, then f(N) < N. If N is divisible by 3, then f(N) ≥ N, with equality only for numbers of the form 2*3^a * m where m is product of primes ≥7 and no factor 5. Also, note that if N is divisible by 3 and not of that special form, then f(N) > N. So the sequence increases when it is divisible by 3 and not fixed. Since it increases, eventually it will become larger than some bound. Could it increase without ever hitting a number with fewer than three proper divisors? Possibly, if it always remains composite and not a prime square. But we need to examine the behavior.

</think>

<solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- The only numbers with at least three proper divisors are composite numbers that are not prime squares.
- The function f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For N divisible by 6, the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/4, so f(N)=13N/12.
- This leads to an increasing sequence for numbers divisible by 6 and not fixed points. By iterating, we can show that the orbit eventually becomes constant at a fixed point of the form 2*3^a*m where m is a product of primes ≥7 (and no factor 5). The starting values that work include all such fixed points and also numbers that map to them in a finite number of steps. The problem is to determine all possible a_1; a complete characterization seems to be that a_1 is a positive integer for which the forward orbit never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. This is a large set that includes all numbers of the form 2*3^a*m (with m product of primes ≥7) and also many numbers that eventually reach such numbers. The answer likely is: all positive integers that are not prime, not prime squares, and not of the form 2^e * 3^b * t where t has a factor 5? But this is not rigorous.
Given the time constraints, I cannot produce a complete rigorous proof. The strongest partial proof would be the analysis of the dynamics: if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence quickly becomes prime or prime square and fails. If a_1 is a multiple of 6 but not fixed, then the sequence increases and eventually becomes a fixed point. The possible a_1 are exactly those numbers that are in the forward orbit of some fixed point. The set of fixed points is {6 * odd not multiple of 5}. Then a_1 can be any number in the forward orbit of these fixed points under f. This set is infinite and can be described recursively: all numbers that are not prime, not prime squares, and that are in the union of the sets of numbers that map to a fixed point in a finite number of steps. This is not a closed form but can be computed. The problem likely expects a description like: a_1 is any positive integer that is not a prime, not a prime square, and for which the sequence defined is infinite. This is essentially the set of numbers that are not "bad". I cannot complete.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is incomplete and lacks rigor. The partial analysis shows that numbers not divisible by 6 likely fail, but we did not prove that all numbers divisible by 6 eventually work (some may hit a prime square or prime before reaching a fixed point). The characterization is not explicit. The strongest steps are the analysis of f for numbers divisible by 6 and the observation that the fixed points are numbers with exactly one factor of 2 and no factor 5. However, we did not rigorously prove that the orbit of any number divisible by 6 never hits a bad number; we only gave examples. The proof is not acceptable.
</self_evaluation>
